Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/medium/am-2018-02768e_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/large/am-2018-02768e_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b02768&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Developing a high-performance, low-cost, and safer rechargeable battery is a primary challenge in next-generation electrochemical energy storage. In this work, a quasi-solid-state (QSS) sodium-ion full battery (SIFB) is designed and fabricated. Hard carbon cloth derived from cotton cloth and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (NVPOF) are employed as the anode and the cathode, respectively, and a sodium ion-conducting gel-polymer membrane is used as both the QSS electrolyte and separator, accomplishing the high energy and power densities in the QSS sodium-ion batteries. The energy density can reach 460 W h kg<sup>–1</sup> according to the mass of the cathode materials. Moreover, the fabricated QSS SIFB also exhibits an excellent rate performance (e.g., about 78.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> specific capacity at 10 C) and a superior cycle performance (e.g., ∼90% capacity retention after 500 cycles at 10 C). These results show that the developed QSS SIFB is a hopeful candidate for large-scale energy storage.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D17903%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJin-Zhi%2BGuo%252C%2BAi-Bo%2BYang%252C%2BZhen-Yi%2BGu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D21%26contentID%3Dacsami.8b02768%26title%3DQuasi-Solid-State%2BSodium-Ion%2BFull%2BBattery%2Bwith%2BHigh-Power%252FEnergy%2BDensities%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D17910%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrochemical%20cells" class="article__tags__link">Electrochemical cells</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292120&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrolytes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrolytes" class="article__tags__link">Electrolytes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Materials" class="article__tags__link">Materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Full+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20full%20battery">sodium-ion full battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Quasi-solid-state&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_quasi-solid-state">quasi-solid-state</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Gel-polymer+Electrolyte&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_gel-polymer%20electrolyte">gel-polymer electrolyte</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=High+Energy+And+Power+Densities&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high%20energy%20and%20power%20densities">high energy and power densities</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na3v22o2f+Cathode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na3V2%28PO4%292O2F%20cathode">Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F cathode</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">On account of the finite nature of fossil fuels and the rising concerns about environmental pollution, studies on storage for clean and renewable energy are more important than ever before. Efficient energy storage technology is an important component in the exploitation of sustainable energy resources. The large-scale energy storage systems (ESSs) on the basis of secondary batteries have drawn much attention for their high energy conversion efficiency, adjustable power, long cycle life, and low cost. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> Although lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been rapidly developed and extensively applied, the low abundance of lithium resources in the Earth’s crust cannot satisfy the substantial demand for upcoming larger-scale ESSs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 3 to 6">(3−6)</a> Thus, it is greatly necessary to develop new inexpensive and efficient ESSs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are hopeful options because of identical working principle as LIBs; and there are highly abundant resources and widespread geological distribution of sodium. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 8 to 12">(8−12)</a> During the past years, numerous trials have been devoted to developing suitable electrode materials for SIBs. Recently, the reliability and practicability of sodium-ion full batteries (SIFBs) have been paid much attention, and great efforts have also been dedicated to evaluating the SIFBs, including Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>//Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> Sb//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14,15)</a> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> symmetric, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> Sb//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (NVPOF), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>//VOPO<sub>4</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> hard carbon//NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> and so on. However, these full cells are still confronted with several challenges, such as low-operating voltage, inferior rate capability, short-term cycle life, and low-power/energy densities. To construct advanced SIFBs with a high working voltage and a high energy density, the choice of superior cathode material should be the most vital because the practicable anode is very limited and almost fixed as carbon materials. Carbonaceous materials have been generally studied as negative electrode materials for SIBs by reason of numerous edge/defective sites and large layer spacing for Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction, especially the hard carbon with the reversible capacity of about 300 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and a low operating potential (almost zero vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), and have been testified to markedly enhance the electrochemical performance of SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 21 and 22">(21,22)</a> Among all cathode materials for SIBs, fluorophosphates NVPOF with the crystal structure of a Na-superionic conductor (NASICON) should be the most promising cathode candidate for SIFBs because of their stable three-dimensional crystalline structure, high discharge plateaux at about 4.01 and 3.60 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, and good thermal stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 18, 23 and 24">(18,23,24)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">In spite of its merit of low price, the security issues confronted for LIBs also exist in SIBs because of the utilization of easily leaked and flammable organic liquid electrolytes, especially in large-scale ESSs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 25 and 26">(25,26)</a> The polymer electrolytes, usually divided into solid and gel electrolytes, should be the most promising candidates of electrolytes for safe and flexible rechargeable batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 27 to 29">(27−29)</a> The solid-polymer electrolytes have been vastly explored because of their higher security compared with the gel-polymer electrolytes (GPEs). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a> However, they are still confronted with several challenges at the present stage, including low ionic conductivity at an ambient temperature and poor contact/interfacial properties (high interfacial resistance and poor/unstable contact with electrodes). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> Nevertheless, the GPEs, which possess the hybrid characteristics combining polymer with liquid electrolytes, have attracted extensive attention because of higher ionic conductivity, broad electrochemical window, good thermodynamic stability, and a higher electrolyte uptake than conventional separators. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34 ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 34 to 36">(34−36)</a> Hence, an efficient and compromised method to solve the safety issues is by achieving quasi-solid-state (QSS, i.e., gel state, an intermediate state between liquid state and all solid state) storage devices using QSS electrolytes.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we design a novel QSS SIFB based on a hard cotton cloth (CC) derived from cotton cloth as the binder-free anode and NVPOF that we recently reported as the cathode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> A sodium ion-conducting gel polymer based on poly(vinylidenefluoride-<i>co</i>-hexafluoropropylene) [P(VDF-HFP)] membrane can serve as both the electrolyte and separator. For convenience, the as-fabricated QSS SIFBs are written as “CC//NVPOF” below. The designed CC//NVPOF exhibited the promising electrochemical characteristics, such as a high energy density of ∼460 W h kg<sup>–1</sup> (calculated from the mass of cathode materials) with a high average operating voltage of about 3.80 V, superior rate capability, and excellent cycling performance. Obviously, a superior electrochemical performance is demonstrated in the P(VDF-HFP) GPE because of the superfast ion transport within NVPOF, which was disclosed by the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) and cyclic voltammetry (CV) studies. The unique battery system pushes the power/energy densities of SIBs to a high level and is a hopeful candidate for large-scale energy storage applications.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Preparation of Sodium Ion-Conducting Gel-Polymer Electrolyte</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The P(VDF-HFP) membrane is based on the literature reported with minor modifications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Typically, P(VDF-HFP) was added into a solution, which contains <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethyl formamide and distilled water in a ratio of 17:1 by weight percent. After treated in a water bath at 80 °C, a watch glass was employed to be the next holder for the solution by providing a smooth surface for the spreading of the solution. Then, the watch glass was immersed in water with a constant temperature at 80 °C, resulting in the successful formation of a homogeneous white membrane. After being dried in a vacuum oven at 100 °C, the dried membrane was cut into circular pieces of diameter 15 mm. Finally, after soaking in an organic electrolyte (1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> solution with propylene carbonate and fluorinated ethylene carbonate at a ratio of 95:5 in volume) for over 12 h in a glovebox, the GPE was successfully prepared for further electrochemical measurements.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Preparation of the NVPOF Material</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The NVPOF cathode material was prepared according to our recent reports. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a></div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Preparation of the CC Anode Derived from Cotton Cloth</h3><div class="NLM_p last">To prepare the CC anode, a commercially available cotton cloth was calcined at 1200 °C for 2 h under an Ar atmosphere. The CC anode was washed in hydrochloric acid solution and distilled water several times and dried at 80 °C.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">X-ray diffraction (XRD) test was used to analyze the crystal texture and purity of the sample in the 2θ range of 10–80°. The microscopic morphology and structure were studied by using scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Hitachi SU8000). Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA, Pyris Diamond TG/DTA, PerkinElmer) was carried out under a N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere in the temperature range of 30–300 °C, with a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">2.5.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The counter/reference electrode and electrolyte in the CR2032 coin-type half-cell were made of Na metal and P(VDF-HFP) GPE, respectively. The NVPOF electrode consisted of the active material, acetylene, and carboxymethylcellulose dissolved in deionized water and covered on an aluminium foil in the ratio of 7:2:1 in weight percent. Then, the electrode was dried in a vacuum oven at 60 °C overnight. The loading density of the active material on the electrodes is about 2.0–2.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The prepared CC was directly used as a binder-free work electrode. Galvanostatic charging/discharging (GCD) tests were carried out on the LAND CT2001A in a potential range of 3.0–4.3 and 0.01–2 V for half-cells. A CV test of varied scan rates from 0.03 to 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> was employed on a VersaSTAT 3 (Princeton Applied Research) electrochemical station. A GITT analyser was used, in which the cells were charged/discharged in the potential window of 3.0–4.3 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at a rate of 0.04 C. Galvanostatic current was applied for 1 h, followed by 6 h of rest. Full cells were assembled by the matching capacity of the NVPOF cathode with the CC anode in excess of 5–10% of anode capacity. The mass of the cathode material was used to calculate the specific capacities of full cells. A pre-sodiation for the CC anode was employed to activate the material and stabilize the electrode surface before the assembling of CC//NVPOF full cells.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The P(VDF-HFP) polymer membrane was synthesized via a simple method that was described previously. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> displays the composition and morphology of the P(VDF-HFP) membrane by powder XRD test, optical photos, and SEM. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows the XRD pattern of the porous P(VDF-HFP) membrane; obviously, there are two intense peaks at 18.14° and 19.64°, which is consistent with the literature reports. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b reveals the optical photographs of the P(VDF-HFP) membrane (top) and its GPE (bottom). A transparent GPE was obtained after soaking in the liquid electrolyte, demonstrating that the liquid electrolyte was resoundingly absorbed into the pores of the P(VDF-HFP) substrate. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c,d shows the SEM images of the surface morphology of the P(VDF-HFP) membrane. On the surface of the membrane, there were abundant pores (<i>d</i> = 1–2 μm) that is advantageous for absorbing and retaining the liquid electrolyte. The average thickness of the membrane was measured by a micrometer caliper and was found to be around 170 μm. The porosity of the membrane was found to be around 68%. Its liquid electrolyte uptake was up to 203%, and the membrane demonstrated good retainability for the liquid electrolyte compared with the common separator of the glass filter; more detailed data are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. Such a high liquid electrolyte uptake is because of the high porosity of the P(VDF-HFP) substrate. Although we prepared the P(VDF-HFP) membrane based on the literature report, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> we optimized the synthesis conditions and the as-prepared P(VDF-HFP) membrane with the best characterization. Besides, we also measured the ionic conductivity of the GEP by the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy method, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>. The ionic conductivity of the P(VDF-HFP) GPE was about 0.42 mS cm<sup>–1</sup> at room temperature. Furthermore, the thermal stability of the P(VDF-HFP) GPE was measured by TGA tests. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, P(VDF-HFP) GPE exhibits the best thermal stability compared with the glass fiber membrane and the commercial separator (NW1640) containing the same amount of electrolyte as P(VDF-HFP) GPE; that is, the evaporation rate of the organic electrolyte in the P(VDF-HFP) GPE is the slowest, demonstrating much enhanced retainability for the liquid electrolyte in the P(VDF-HFP) GPE than in the common separators.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/medium/am-2018-02768e_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/large/am-2018-02768e_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Structural and morphology characterizations of the P(VDF-HFP) polymer membrane. (a) XRD pattern, (b) optical photograph, and (c,d) SEM images.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/large/am-2018-02768e_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b02768&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The NVPOF cathode material was prepared via a controlled hydrothermal method, as recently reported by us. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a</a> is the XRD pattern of the NVPOF cathode material, indicating that all characteristic peaks of the material can be well-indexed to the NASICON-type NVPOF of <i>I</i>4/<i>mmm</i> space group. The SEM images in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4b</a> displays that the length and width of the NVPOF material is about 400 and 200 nm, respectively. This well coincides with our previous studies. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> is the XRD pattern and SEM image of the CC anode. As disclosed, the XRD pattern exhibits broad peaks at 20° and 43°, which corresponds to the lattice planes of (002) and (100) in the disordered carbon. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b</a>, the CC anode is composed of interwoven mesh of hollow microtubules, and the diameter of hollow microtubule is around 10–15 μm, which coincides well with our previous reports. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical performance of the NVPOF cathode is first evaluated by GCD tests in half-cells with the P(VDF-HFP) GPE as both electrolyte and separator. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a shows the outstanding rate capabilities of NVPOF with a capacity of 125.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 C, and the corresponding initial Coulombic efficiency is ∼90.35%. This value is very close to 127.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in the liquid electrolyte recently reported by us, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> suggesting the high ionic conductivity of the P(VDF-HFP) GPE. Even at 10 C, it still delivers a capacity of ∼84 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. This value equates to a capacity retention of ∼72% compared to the specific capacity at 0.1 C, indicating that only 28% of the specific capacity is cut back even though the rate augments 100 times. The GCD curves at different rates (inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a) disclose a clear charge/discharge plateau even at 10 C. We also analyzed the polarization phenomenon in the whole range of rates. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>, the NVPOF cathode reveals the minor polarization even at 10 C. The NVPOF cathode also exhibits outstanding cycling performance at different rates of 0.2 and 1 C. After 200 cycles at a relatively low rate of 0.2 C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b), the NVPOF achieves a capacity retention of above 95% with the capacity decay from the initial 118.9–113.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c gives its cycling performance at 1 C; as shown, the capacity retention is still up to 94.6% after 1500 cycles, implying the superior cycling stability of the NVPOF electrode in the P(VDF-HFP) GPE. This finding is also verified by the crystal structure and morphology tests after cycling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). The capacity fading rates per cycle was only 0.0036% at 1 C. Notably, the above data are superior to the previous reports that were studied in the liquid electrolyte by us. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a></div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/medium/am-2018-02768e_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/large/am-2018-02768e_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Electrochemical performance of the NVPOF cathode in half-cells. (a) Rate capability and the corresponding GCD curves (inset). (b) Cycling performances and the corresponding GCD curves (inset) at 0.2 C. (c) Long cycling stability with Coulombic efficiency over 1500 cycles at 1 C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/large/am-2018-02768e_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b02768&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To analyze the reaction kinetics of the NVPOF electrode during Na<sup>+</sup> de-/intercalation processes in the P(VDF-HFP) GPE, CV and GITT tests were employed in the NVPOF half-cells. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, CV curves were recorded in the voltage window of 3.0–4.3 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at various scanning rates from 0.1 to 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. With the increase of the scanning rate, the peak currents also increase continuously, at the same time the cathodic or anodic peaks shift to lower or higher potentials. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b shows the linear fitting profiles between the peak current densities (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) and the square root of scanning rates (<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>). The excellent fitting results and the high <i>R</i> values imply that the electrochemical Na-intercalation/extraction reactions are diffusion-controlled processes. Consequently, the apparent Na-diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub>) can be counted according to the Randles–Sevcik <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/medium/am-2018-02768e_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>where <i>i</i><sub>p</sub> is the peak current of cathodic or anodic peaks, <i>n</i> is the number of electrons per molecule during the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation (<i>n</i> = 2 for V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox pair), <i>A</i> is the surface area of the electrode, <i>C</i><sub>0</sub> is the concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrode (0.0077 mol cm<sup>–3</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> and <i>v</i> is the scanning rate. The <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values are 6.50 × 10<sup>–13</sup>, 6.84 × 10<sup>–13</sup>, 8.51 × 10<sup>–13</sup>, and 9.74 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> for peaks A1, C1, A2, and C2, respectively. Evidently, the low-potential A1/C1 redox couple yields smaller <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values than those of the high-potential couple, suggesting that Na intercalation/extraction at a low potential of around 3.5 V is relatively sluggish and requires higher energy, which is a common result and consistent with previous reports in the liquid electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/medium/am-2018-02768e_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/large/am-2018-02768e_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Studies of Na migration kinetics for the NVPOF electrode in the P(VDF-HFP) GPE. (a) CV profiles at various scan rates from 0.03 to 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Linear fitting for the relationship between <i>i</i><sub>p</sub> and <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> from CV profiles. (c) GITT test results showing the changes of <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> and quasi-equilibrium potentials along with the GCD processes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/large/am-2018-02768e_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b02768&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">GITT was measured at 0.04 C over the potential window of 3.0–4.3 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na in first cycle. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a> shows the applied current flux and the resulting voltage profile for a single titration during the first charging and discharging process of the NVPOF electrode in the P(VDF-HFP) GPE. A straight-line behavior is observed between the cell voltage <i>E</i> and the square root of galvanostatic time τ<sup>1/2</sup>, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8b,d</a>, from which <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values can be counted according to the following <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/medium/am-2018-02768e_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>where <i>m</i><sub>B</sub>, <i>M</i><sub>B</sub>, and <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> are the mass, molecular weight, and molar volume of the NVPOF, respectively; τ is the time for an applied galvanostatic current; <i>S</i> is the surface area of the electrode; <i>L</i> is the average radius of the material particles; and Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> are the change of the steady-state voltage and cell voltage <i>E</i> during the current pulse, respectively. The evolution of quasi-equilibrium potential and calculated <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values along with the GCD curves are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c. Notably, each quasi-equilibrium potential (the red squares in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c) is extremely close to the GCD curves, indicating that the Na intercalation/extraction processes are kinetically fast with high ionic and electronic conductivities. Form the GITT tests, the values of <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> are in the 10<sup>–11</sup> to 10<sup>–12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> order of magnitude, and a similar tendency is observed at both charging and discharging processes. Comparing the results of CV, the values of <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> obtained from GITT are higher, because the actually measured value of Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> in GITT is large. Another reason could be the thermodynamic factor, which gives rise to the deviation from the ideal crystallized material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> As is well-known, the strain and surface energy can make for the formation energy of the real material, which can affect the open circuit voltage and may give rise to a varied Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> value during the entire charging/discharging processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> Furthermore, the <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values at the upper plateau are obviously larger than those at the lower plateau, indicating that Na intercalation/extraction is relatively sluggish and requires higher energy at a low potential of around 3.60 V, and in the middle of each plateau, the values are clearly smaller than those at the both ends, which is a common result and consistent with previous reports in the liquid electrolyte <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> and other electrode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref44" aria-label="reference 40 and 44">(40,44)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">As discussed above, the P(VDF-HFP) GPE should be a promising electrolyte with high ionic conductivity. Hence, a QSS SIFB using the prepared NVPOF as the cathode, CC as the flexible and self-supporting anode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S5 and S9</a>), and the P(VDF-HFP) GPE as both electrolyte and separator was assembled and studied. Meanwhile, we also evaluated the performance of the CC anode in the P(VDF-HFP) GPE, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>. The CC anode exhibits superior cycling stability with a capacity retention of more than 85% after 100 cycles at 0.5 C (corresponding current density of 65 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), and the Coulombic efficiency is ∼77.29%. A schematic illustration of the assembled QSS SIFB is displayed in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a. The typical GCD curves of the NVPOF cathode and the CC anode were performed at 0.1 C in the half-cell, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a. Evidently, there are two pairs of excellent oxidation/reduction plateaux at 4.03/4.02 and 3.63/3.62 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na in the NVPOF cathode, whereas the average discharge/charge plateau of the CC anode is about 0.06/0.08 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. On the basis of the GCD tests, it can be rationally deduced that the average voltage output of the assembled CC//NVPOF full cells will be about 3.80 V, as already testified by GCD curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b). In the GCD curves, the discharge capacity is about 121.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 C with an initial Coulombic efficiency of 93.66%. As a result, the energy density delivered by CC//NVPOF is ∼460 W h kg<sup>–1</sup>, calculated on the mass of the NVPOF cathode. Therefore, the energy density of the final commercialized CC//NVPOF can reach ∼184 W h kg<sup>–1</sup>, if the mass percentage of the cathode material is about 40% in the whole battery. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c is the representative CV profiles tested at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Obviously, there are two couples of anodic/cathodic peaks at 4.03/3.94 V and 3.62/3.50 V, and the average working voltage is ∼3.80 V in the discharging processes, which is well identical to the predicted value in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/medium/am-2018-02768e_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/large/am-2018-02768e_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) GCD curves of both CC and NVPOF electrodes at 0.1 C in half-cells; the inset is the schematic illustration of CC//NVPOF full cells. The performance of the CC//NVPOF full cells: (b) GCD curves at 0.1 C and (c) CV patterns of initial three cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/large/am-2018-02768e_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b02768&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further evaluate the electrochemical performance of the assembled CC//NVPOF full cells, the rate and cycling performances were investigated by GCD tests at various rates. Shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a, the rate capability was obtained from 0.1 to 10 C, exhibiting an outstanding high-rate performance. Even at 10 C, the delivered discharge capacity is still 78.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, with a capacity retention of ∼65% compared to the specific capacity at 0.1 C. The corresponding GCD curves of CC//NVPOF at different rates are further exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b; there are still two clear discharge plateaux of about 3.75 and 3.2 V at 10 C. Furthermore, the specific capacity at 0.1 C can well restore to its initial values after the rate tests of 45 cycles, implying that the CC//NVPOF full cells can work at arbitrarily varied current densities. This outcome promotes the capability of CC//NVPOF full cells to store unstable and intermittent energy sources, for instance, wind and solar energies. More importantly, the CC//NVPOF full cells also exhibit outstanding cycling performance, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c. When cycled at 1 C, the CC//NVPOF full cell delivers an initial capacity of 116.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with a capacity retention of about 92.2% after 200 cycles. To test the much longer cycle life, the CC//NVPOF full cell was further cycled at 10 C, exhibiting a capacity retention of about 90% even after 500 cycles.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/medium/am-2018-02768e_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/large/am-2018-02768e_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical performance of the CC//NVPOF full cells. Rate capability (a) and the corresponding GCD curves (b). (c) Cycle stabilities at different rates of 1 C for 200 cycles and 10 C for 500 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/large/am-2018-02768e_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b02768&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In addition, we summarized and compared many recently reported QSS full-cell configurations based on the SIBs and LIBs in regard to their energy and power densities, as presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>. As seen in the Ragone plots, CC//NVPOF possesses a high energy density of almost 460 W h kg<sup>–1</sup> at a power density of 52 W kg<sup>–1</sup>, which is notably higher than those of the majority of reported ones, except for the lithium-based oxides graphite//Li(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Co<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> Although the energy densities of CC//NVPOF are slightly lower than those of graphite//Li(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Co<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub> at a power density of &lt;300 W kg<sup>–1</sup>, it exhibits markedly superior power performance. Moreover, the power/energy densities of CC//NVPOF are notably higher than that of Sb//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> By comparison, our CC//NVPOF reveals outstanding electrochemical performances, and more details are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/medium/am-2018-02768e_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/large/am-2018-02768e_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Ragone plots for the CC//NVPOF full cells compared with other literature works on QSS full cells. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref45 ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref45 ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 11 and 45 to 47">(11,45−47)</a></p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/asset/images/large/am-2018-02768e_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b02768&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, a QSS SIFB CC//NVPOF has been fabricated based on the CC as the binder-free anode and NVPOF as the cathode, using a P(VDF-HFP) GPE as both the electrolyte and separator. Such a full cell can exhibit a high energy density of ∼460 W h kg<sup>–1</sup> (calculated based on the mass of the NVPOF) with a high average operating voltage of above 3.80 V. Electrochemical tests demonstrate that the assembled CC//NVPOF exhibits outstanding electrochemical performance in the aspect of excellent rate capability and ultralong cycling stability, illustrating that the designed CC//NVPOF would be a hopeful candidate for advanced secondary batteries with high-power/energy densities. More importantly, the GITT and CV tests were implemented to analyze the Na-migration kinetics of the NVPOF electrode in the P(VDF-HFP) GPE during the Na<sup>+</sup> de-/intercalation processes.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.8b02768" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.8b02768</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Characterization for the P(VDF-HFP) GPE; XRD patterns and SEM images for the NVPOF cathode and the CC anode; single GITT profile for the NVPOF cathode; electrochemical performance for the CC anode; and comparison of electrochemical properties (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b02768/suppl_file/am8b02768_si_001.pdf">am8b02768_si_001.pdf (598.75 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b02768" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrZwDymuubZm7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgNPkln1cWGhA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhsVGktL%252FJ&amp;md5=5035bfda7631ad9d075e8836e61146ef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201410376"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kundu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Talaie, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Duffort, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nazar, L.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A High-Energy Lithium-Ion Capacitor by Integration of a 3D Interconnected Titanium Carbide Nanoparticle Chain Anode with a Pyridine-Derived Porous Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Cathode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Huanwen; Zhang, Yu; Ang, Huixiang; Zhang, Yongqi; Tan, Hui Teng; Zhang, Yufei; Guo, Yuanyuan; Franklin, Joseph B.; Wu, Xing Long; Srinivasan, Madhavi; Fan, Hong Jin; Yan, Qingyu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-ion capacitors (LICs) are hybrid energy storage devices that have the potential to bridge the gap between conventional high-energy lithium-ion batteries and high-power capacitors by combining their complementary features.  The challenge for LICs was to improve the energy storage at high charge-discharge rates by circumventing the discrepancy in kinetics between the intercalation anode and capacitive cathode.  The rational design of new nanostructured LIC electrodes that both exhibit a dominating capacitive mechanism (both double layer and pseudocapacitive) with a diminished intercalation process, is reported.  Specifically, the electrodes are a 3-dimensional interconnected TiC nanoparticle chain anode, synthesized by carbothermal conversion of graphene/TiO2 hybrid aerogels, and a pyridine-derived hierarchical porous nitrogen-doped carbon (PHPNC) cathode.  Electrochem. properties of both electrodes are thoroughly characterized which demonstrate their outstanding high-rate capabilities.  The fully assembled PHPNC//TiC LIC device delivers an energy d. of 101.5 Wh/kg and a power d. of 67.5 kW/kg (achieved at 23.4 Wh/kg), and a reasonably good cycle stability (≈82% retention after 5000 cycles) within the voltage range of 0.0-4.5 V.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoMyLfEXsVVTLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljgDt0yeYf49A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xjs1ejt78%253D&amp;md5=84636d13db8d9d26033becb2b5edb0e2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, H.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, L.-Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, R.-S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">New Development of Lithium-Sulfur Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Slater, Michael D.; Kim, Donghan; Lee, Eungje; Doeff, Marca; Johnson, Christopher S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojsFXLPEQzILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljgDt0yeYf49A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XntlSnurc%253D&amp;md5=fa5d7d089d261a40896cb6b03e88bfbe</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1039/c1ee01782a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ong, S.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">To evaluate the potential of Na-ion batteries, we contrast in this work the difference between Na-ion and Li-ion based intercalation chemistries in terms of three key battery properties-voltage, phase stability and diffusion barriers.  The compds. investigated comprise the layered AMO2 and AMS2 structures, the olivine and maricite AMPO4 structures, and the NASICON A3V2(PO4)3 structures.  The calcd. Na voltages for the compds. investigated are 0.18-0.57 V lower than that of the corresponding Li voltages, in agreement with previous exptl. data.  We believe the obsd. lower voltages for Na compds. are predominantly a cathodic effect related to the much smaller energy gain from inserting Na into the host structure compared to inserting Li.  We also found a relatively strong dependence of battery properties on structural features.  In general, the difference between the Na and Li voltage of the same structure, ΔVNa-Li, is less neg. for the maricite structures preferred by Na, and more neg. for the olivine structures preferred by Li.  The layered compds. have the most neg. ΔVNa-Li.  In terms of phase stability, we found that open structures, such as the layered and NASICON structures, that are better able to accommodate the larger Na+ ion generally have both Na and Li versions of the same compd.  For the close-packed AMPO4 structures, our results show that Na generally prefers the maricite structure, while Li prefers the olivine structure, in agreement with previous exptl. work.  We also found surprising evidence that the barriers for Na+ migration can potentially be lower than that for Li+ migration in the layered structures.  Overall, our findings indicate that Na-ion systems can be competitive with Li-ion systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGosFyTQ7K1B3LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixrgPD8SgBOQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXht1CqsLzK&amp;md5=5a6e72a3ae5ebf92d84322240b2c29cc</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                 10.1021/cr500192f</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D114%26publication_year%3D2014%26pages%3D11636-11682%26journal%3DChem.%2BRev.%26author%3DN.%2BYabuuchi%26author%3DK.%2BKubota%26author%3DM.%2BDahbi%26author%3DS.%2BKomaba%26title%3DResearch%2BDevelopment%2Bon%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fcr500192f&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b02768&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fcr500192f&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Kubota, Kei; Dahbi, Mouad; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of sodium-ion battery technol.  Future outlook of sodium-ion batteries is discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo_Iht8id70u7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixrgPD8SgBOQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvFSiu7bL&amp;md5=d4497339c61083b2ec2c0568261e4909</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1039/c2ee02781j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palomares, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Serras, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Villaluenga, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hueso, K.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5884-5901</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRYtxfVVW_sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixrgPD8SgBOQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XivFWnu7s%253D&amp;md5=72869139086b8ee9adec746418ff84b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201702093"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chao, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, H.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A sustainable iron-based sodium ion battery of porous carbon-Fe3O4/Na2FeP2O7 with high performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ming, Jun; Ming, Hai; Yang, Wenjing; Kwak, Won-Jin; Park, Jin-Bum; Zheng, Junwei; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">RSC Advances</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A type of porous carbon-Fe3O4 (e.g., PC-Fe3O4) composite with an industrially scalable prodn. was introduced in the sodium ion battery application for the first time.  The PC-Fe3O4 composite, consisting of highly dispersed Fe3O4 nanocrystals within the porous carbon with a relatively low wt. percent of 45.5 wt%, could efficiently demonstrate high capacities of 225, 168, 127, 103, 98 and 90 mA h g-1 under the current densities of 50, 100, 200, 300, 400 and 500 mA g-1 with a good stability over 400 cycles.  The utilization co-efficient of Fe3O4 nanocrystals was proven to be much higher than most of the Fe3O4 nanoparticles reported recently via the study of the capacity contribution of carbon originally.  In addn., the robustness of electrode during the charge-discharge was well characterized by ex situ XRD and emission SEM (SEM).  More importantly, a new concept of an elemental iron-based sodium ion battery of PC-Fe3O4/Na2FeP2O7 is presented.  This is the first example to introduce an element-rich configuration in the sodium ion battery from the viewpoint of sustainability.  The full battery demonstrated a superior capacity of 93 mA h g-1, high capacity retention of 93.3% over 100 cycles and work voltage around 2.28 V with the energy d. of 203 W h kg-1.  Such configuration of an iron-based sodium battery would be highly promising and sustainable owing to its low cost and high stability in grid storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqlfLDULELEl7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljrUx4KALLC4Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXitFehtb7J&amp;md5=dbdb474ad5240a7412c37b8740147f9c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201600467"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goodenough, J.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In Situ Binding Sb Nanospheres on Graphene via Oxygen Bonds as Superior Anode for Ultrafast Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wan, Fang; Guo, Jin-Zhi; Zhang, Xiao-Hua; Zhang, Jing-Ping; Sun, Hai-Zhu; Yan, Qingyu; Han, Dong-Xue; Niu, Li; Wu, Xing-Long</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7790-7799</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphene incorporation should be one effective strategy to develop advanced electrode materials for a sodium-ion battery (SIB).  Herein, the micro/nanostructural Sb/graphene composite (Sb-O-G) is successfully prepd. with the uniform Sb nanospheres (∼100 nm) bound on the graphene via oxygen bonds.  It is revealed that the in-situ-constructed oxygen bonds play a significant role on enhancing Na-storage properties, esp. the ultrafast charge/discharge capability.  The oxygen-bond-enhanced Sb-O-G composite can deliver a high capacity of 220 mAh/g at an ultrahigh c.d. of 12 A/g, which is obviously superior to the similar Sb/G composite (130 mAh/g at 10 A/g) just without Sb-O-C bonds.  It also exhibits the highest Na-storage capacity compared to Sb/G and pure Sb nanoparticles as well as the best cycling performance.  More importantly, this Sb-O-G anode achieves ultrafast (120 C) energy storage in SIB full cells, which have already been shown to power a 26-bulb array and calculator.  All of these superior performances originate from the structural stability of Sb-O-C bonds during Na uptake/release, which has been verified by ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopies and IR spectroscopies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqAWqDAYuZl6rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljrUx4KALLC4Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XjvFylur8%253D&amp;md5=20ff4444ec296955ad0cbb06a02dfbef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201505943"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kopold, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">van Aken, P.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A rather general strategy to prep. self-supported interpenetrating 3D tricontinuous cathodes that is based on the facile electrostatic spray deposition (ESD) technique is proposed.  Taking Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP) as a model system, interconnected 3D tricontinuous NVP:rGO-CNT (reduced graphene oxide-carbon nanotube) is directly deposited on the current collector without any conductive additives or binders usually needed for battery assembling.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Effect of Carbon Matrix Dimensions on the Electrochemical Properties of Na3V2(PO4)3 Nanograins for High-Performance Symmetric Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Shuo; Dong, Yifan; Xu, Lin; Xu, Xu; He, Liang; Mai, Liqiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. properties of Na3V2(PO4)3 nanograins having different carbon contents were investigated by a one-step solid state reaction and evaluated in different electrolyte systems.  It was found that the sodium storage performance in terms of capacity, coulombic efficiency and cyclability can be significantly improved in a new sodium bis(fluorosulfonyl) imide/propylene carbonate electrolyte which is introduced for the first time, bringing this material close to viability as a promising cathode for room-temp. sodium-ion batteries.  The mechanism of sodium insertion/extn. into/from Na3V2(PO4)3 lattice is analyzed by in-situ XRD technique.
